	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en"><i>Messiah</i> (Handel)</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Oratorio by Handel</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>
<table class="infobox vevent haudio" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="summary album" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold"><i>Messiah</i></th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-weight:bold;"><a href="/wiki/Oratorio" title="Oratorio">Oratorio</a> by <a href="/wiki/George_Frideric_Handel" title="George Frideric Handel">George Frideric Handel</a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/File:Messiah-titlepage.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Messiah-titlepage.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Messiah-titlepage.jpg/220px-Messiah-titlepage.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="180" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Messiah-titlepage.jpg/330px-Messiah-titlepage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/71/Messiah-titlepage.jpg 2x" data-file-width="361" data-file-height="295" /></a><div>Title page of Handel's autograph score</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Text</th><td><a href="/wiki/Charles_Jennens" title="Charles Jennens">Charles Jennens</a>, from the <a href="/wiki/Authorized_King_James_Version" class="mw-redirect" title="Authorized King James Version">King James Bible</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Composed</th><td><span class="vevent">22&#160;August&#160;1741<span style="display:none">&#160;(<span class="bday dtstart published updated">1741-08-22</span>)</span>&#160;–&#32;14&#160;September&#160;1741<span style="display:none">&#160;(<span class="dtend">1741-09-14</span>)</span>: <span class="location summary"> London</span></span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Movements</th><td>53 in three parts</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Vocal</th><td><a href="/wiki/SATB" title="SATB"><abbr title="soprano, alto, tenor and bass">SATB</abbr></a> choir and solo</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Instrumental</th><td><div class="hlist hlist-separated"><ul><li>2 trumpets</li><li>timpani</li><li>2 oboes</li><li>2 violins</li><li>viola</li><li>basso continuo</li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><i><b>Messiah</b></i> (HWV 56)<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;n 1&#93;</a></sup> is an English-language <a href="/wiki/Oratorio" title="Oratorio">oratorio</a> composed in 1741 by <a href="/wiki/George_Frideric_Handel" title="George Frideric Handel">George Frideric Handel</a>, with a scriptural text compiled by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Jennens" title="Charles Jennens">Charles Jennens</a> from the <a href="/wiki/King_James_Bible" class="mw-redirect" title="King James Bible">King James Bible</a>, and from the <a href="/wiki/Coverdale_Bible" title="Coverdale Bible">Coverdale Psalter</a>, the version of the <i><a href="/wiki/Psalms" title="Psalms">Psalms</a></i> included with the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a></i>. It was first performed in <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a> on 13 April 1742 and received its <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> premiere nearly a year later. After an initially modest public reception, the oratorio gained in popularity, eventually becoming one of the best-known and most frequently performed choral works in Western music.
</p><p>Handel's reputation in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, where he had lived since 1712, had been established through his compositions of Italian opera. He turned to English oratorio in the 1730s in response to changes in public taste; <i>Messiah</i> was his sixth work in this genre. Although its <a href="/wiki/Structure_of_Handel%27s_Messiah" title="Structure of Handel&#39;s Messiah">structure</a> resembles that of <a href="/wiki/Opera#The_Baroque_era" title="Opera">opera</a>, it is not in dramatic form; there are no impersonations of characters and no direct speech. Instead, Jennens's text is an extended reflection on <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">Messiah</a> called <a href="/wiki/Christ_(title)" title="Christ (title)">Christ</a>. The text begins in <a href="/wiki/Messiah_Part_I" title="Messiah Part I">Part I</a> with prophecies by <a href="/wiki/Isaiah" title="Isaiah">Isaiah</a> and others, and moves to the <a href="/wiki/Annunciation_to_the_shepherds" title="Annunciation to the shepherds">annunciation to the shepherds</a>, the only "scene" taken from the <a href="/wiki/Gospel" title="Gospel">Gospels</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Messiah_Part_II" title="Messiah Part II">Part II</a>, Handel concentrates on the <a href="/wiki/Passion_of_Jesus" title="Passion of Jesus">Passion</a> and ends with the "<a href="/wiki/Messiah_Part_II#44" title="Messiah Part II">Hallelujah</a>" chorus. In <a href="/wiki/Messiah_Part_III" title="Messiah Part III">Part III</a> he covers the <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_the_dead#Christianity" title="Resurrection of the dead">resurrection of the dead</a> and Christ's glorification in heaven.
</p><p>Handel wrote <i>Messiah</i> for modest vocal and instrumental forces, with optional settings for many of the individual numbers. In the years after his death, the work was adapted for performance on a much larger scale, with giant orchestras and choirs. In other efforts to update it, its orchestration was revised and amplified by (among others) <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Mozart</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Der_Messias" title="Der Messias">Der Messias</a></i>). In the late 20th and early 21st centuries the trend has been towards reproducing a greater fidelity to Handel's original intentions, although "big <i>Messiah"</i> productions continue to be mounted. A near-complete version was issued on <a href="/wiki/Gramophone_record#78_rpm_disc_developments" class="mw-redirect" title="Gramophone record">78&#160;rpm discs</a> in 1928; since then the work has been recorded many times.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BLW_Handel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/BLW_Handel.jpg/170px-BLW_Handel.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="312" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/BLW_Handel.jpg/255px-BLW_Handel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/BLW_Handel.jpg/340px-BLW_Handel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1775" data-file-height="3261" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BLW_Handel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The statue erected in Handel's honour, in <a href="/wiki/Vauxhall_Gardens" title="Vauxhall Gardens">Vauxhall Gardens</a>, London; now in the <a href="/wiki/Victoria_and_Albert_Museum" title="Victoria and Albert Museum">Victoria and Albert Museum</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The composer <a href="/wiki/George_Frideric_Handel" title="George Frideric Handel">George Frideric Handel</a>, born in <a href="/wiki/Halle_(Saale)" title="Halle (Saale)">Halle</a>, Germany in 1685, took up permanent residence in London in 1712, and became a naturalised British subject in 1727.<sup id="cite_ref-OMO_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OMO-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> By 1741 his pre-eminence in British music was evident from the honours he had accumulated, including a pension from the court of <a href="/wiki/George_II_of_Great_Britain" title="George II of Great Britain">King George II</a>, the office of Composer of Musick for the <a href="/wiki/Chapel_Royal" title="Chapel Royal">Chapel Royal</a>, and—most unusually for a living person—a statue erected in his honour in <a href="/wiki/Vauxhall_Gardens" title="Vauxhall Gardens">Vauxhall Gardens</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Within a large and varied musical output, Handel was a vigorous champion of Italian opera, which he had introduced to London in 1711 with <i><a href="/wiki/Rinaldo_(opera)" title="Rinaldo (opera)">Rinaldo</a></i>. He subsequently wrote and presented more than 40 such operas in London's theatres.<sup id="cite_ref-OMO_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OMO-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By the early 1730s public taste for Italian opera was beginning to fade. The popular success of <a href="/wiki/John_Gay" title="John Gay">John Gay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johann_Christoph_Pepusch" title="Johann Christoph Pepusch">Johann Christoph Pepusch</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Beggar%27s_Opera" title="The Beggar&#39;s Opera">The Beggar's Opera</a></i> (first performed in 1728) had heralded a spate of English-language ballad-operas that mocked the pretensions of Italian opera.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> With box-office receipts falling, Handel's productions were increasingly reliant on private subsidies from the nobility. Such funding became harder to obtain after the launch in 1730 of the <a href="/wiki/Opera_of_the_Nobility" title="Opera of the Nobility">Opera of the Nobility</a>, a rival company to his own. Handel overcame this challenge, but he spent large sums of his own money in doing so.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although prospects for Italian opera were declining, Handel remained committed to the genre, but as alternatives to his staged works he began to introduce English-language oratorios.<sup id="cite_ref-Burrows_1991,_p._4_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burrows_1991,_p._4-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In Rome in 1707–08 he had written two Italian oratorios at a time when opera performances in the city were temporarily forbidden under papal decree.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> His first venture into English oratorio had been <i><a href="/wiki/Esther_(Handel)" title="Esther (Handel)">Esther</a></i>, which was written and performed for a private patron in about 1718.<sup id="cite_ref-Burrows_1991,_p._4_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burrows_1991,_p._4-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In 1732 Handel brought a revised and expanded version of <i>Esther</i> to the <a href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Theatre" title="Her Majesty&#39;s Theatre">King's Theatre, Haymarket</a>, where members of the royal family attended a glittering premiere on 6 May. Its success encouraged Handel to write two more oratorios (<i><a href="/wiki/Deborah_(Handel)" title="Deborah (Handel)">Deborah</a></i> and  <i><a href="/wiki/Athalia_(Handel)" title="Athalia (Handel)">Athalia</a></i>). All three oratorios were performed to large and appreciative audiences at the <a href="/wiki/Sheldonian_Theatre" title="Sheldonian Theatre">Sheldonian Theatre</a> in Oxford in mid-1733. Undergraduates reportedly sold their furniture to raise the money for the five-shilling tickets.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1735 Handel received the text for a new oratorio named <i><a href="/wiki/Saul_(Handel)" title="Saul (Handel)">Saul</a></i> from its <a href="/wiki/Librettist" class="mw-redirect" title="Librettist">librettist</a> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Jennens" title="Charles Jennens">Charles Jennens</a>, a wealthy landowner with musical and literary interests.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Because Handel's main creative concern was still with opera, he did not write the music for <i>Saul</i> until 1738, in preparation for his 1738–39 theatrical season. The work, after opening at the King's Theatre in January 1739 to a warm reception, was quickly followed by the less successful oratorio <i><a href="/wiki/Israel_in_Egypt" title="Israel in Egypt">Israel in Egypt</a></i> (which may also have come from Jennens).<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Although Handel continued to write operas, the trend towards English-language productions became irresistible as the decade ended. After three performances of his last Italian opera <i><a href="/wiki/Deidamia_(opera)" title="Deidamia (opera)">Deidamia</a></i> in January and February 1741, he abandoned the genre.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In July 1741 Jennens sent him a new libretto for an oratorio; in a letter dated 10 July to his friend <a href="/wiki/Edward_Holdsworth" title="Edward Holdsworth">Edward Holdsworth</a>, Jennens wrote: "I hope [Handel] will lay out his whole Genius &amp; Skill upon it, that the Composition may excell all his former Compositions, as the Subject excells every other subject. The Subject is Messiah".<sup id="cite_ref-B10_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B10-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Synopsis">Synopsis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Synopsis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Structure_of_Handel%27s_Messiah" title="Structure of Handel&#39;s Messiah">Structure of Handel's Messiah</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Christian_theology" title="Christian theology">Christian theology</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Messiah#Christianity" title="Messiah">Messiah</a> is the saviour of humankind. The Messiah who is called <a href="/wiki/Christ_(title)" title="Christ (title)">Christ</a>, is identified with the person of <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a>, known by his followers as the Christ or "Jesus Christ". Handel's <i>Messiah</i> has been described by the early-music scholar Richard Luckett as "a commentary on [Jesus Christ's] Nativity, Passion, Resurrection and Ascension", beginning with God's promises as spoken by the prophets and ending with Christ's glorification in heaven.<sup id="cite_ref-L76_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L76-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> In contrast with most of Handel's oratorios, the singers in <i>Messiah</i> do not assume dramatic roles; there is no single, dominant narrative voice; and very little use is made of <a href="/wiki/Direct_speech" title="Direct speech">quoted speech</a>. In his libretto, Jennens's intention was not to dramatise the life and teachings of Jesus, but to acclaim the "Mystery of Godliness",<sup id="cite_ref-B57_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B57-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> using a compilation of extracts from the <a href="/wiki/Authorized_King_James_Version" class="mw-redirect" title="Authorized King James Version">Authorized (King James) Version</a> of the Bible, and from the <a href="/wiki/Psalms" title="Psalms">Psalms</a> included in the 1662 <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The three-part structure of the work approximates to that of Handel's three-act operas, with the "parts" subdivided by Jennens into "<a href="/wiki/Messiah_structure#Scenes" class="mw-redirect" title="Messiah structure">scenes</a>". Each scene is a collection of individual numbers or "movements" which take the form of <a href="/wiki/Recitative" title="Recitative">recitatives</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aria" title="Aria">arias</a> and choruses.<sup id="cite_ref-B57_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B57-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> There are two instrumental numbers, the opening <i>Sinfony</i><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;n 2&#93;</a></sup> in the style of a <a href="/wiki/French_overture" title="French overture">French overture</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Pastorale" title="Pastorale">pastoral</a> <i>Pifa</i>, often called the "pastoral symphony", at the mid-point of Part I.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Part I, the Messiah's coming and the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_birth_of_Jesus" title="Virgin birth of Jesus">virgin birth</a> are predicted by the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> prophets. The <a href="/wiki/Annunciation_to_the_shepherds" title="Annunciation to the shepherds">annunciation to the shepherds</a> of the birth of the Christ is represented in the words of <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Luke" title="Gospel of Luke">Luke's gospel</a>. Part II covers <a href="/wiki/Passion_of_Jesus" title="Passion of Jesus">Christ's passion and his death</a>, his <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_Jesus" title="Resurrection of Jesus">resurrection</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ascension_of_Jesus" title="Ascension of Jesus">ascension</a>, the first spreading of the <a href="/wiki/Gospel" title="Gospel">gospel</a> through the world, and a definitive statement of God's glory summarised in the "Hallelujah". Part III begins with the promise of redemption, followed by a prediction of the <a href="/wiki/Last_Judgment" title="Last Judgment">day of judgment</a> and the "<a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_the_dead" title="Resurrection of the dead">general resurrection</a>", ending with the final victory over sin and death and the acclamation of Christ.<sup id="cite_ref-L79_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L79-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> According to the musicologist <a href="/wiki/Donald_Burrows_(musicologist)" title="Donald Burrows (musicologist)">Donald Burrows</a>, much of the text is so allusive as to be largely incomprehensible to those ignorant of the biblical accounts.<sup id="cite_ref-B57_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B57-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> For the benefit of his audiences Jennens printed and issued a pamphlet explaining the reasons for his choices of scriptural selections.<sup id="cite_ref-Vickers_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vickers-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_history">Writing history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Writing history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Libretto">Libretto</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Libretto">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Jennens23.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Charles_Jennens23.jpg/170px-Charles_Jennens23.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Charles_Jennens23.jpg/255px-Charles_Jennens23.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Charles_Jennens23.jpg/340px-Charles_Jennens23.jpg 2x" data-file-width="404" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Jennens23.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A portrait of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Jennens" title="Charles Jennens">Charles Jennens</a> by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hudson_(painter)" title="Thomas Hudson (painter)">Thomas Hudson</a> from around 1740; now in the <a href="/wiki/Handel_House_Museum" class="mw-redirect" title="Handel House Museum">Handel House Museum</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Charles Jennens was born around 1700, into a prosperous landowning family whose lands and properties in Warwickshire and Leicestershire he eventually inherited.<sup id="cite_ref-MT-Jennens_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MT-Jennens-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> His religious and political views—he opposed the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Settlement_1701" title="Act of Settlement 1701">Act of Settlement of 1701</a> which secured the accession to the British throne for the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Hanover" title="House of Hanover">House of Hanover</a>—prevented him from receiving his degree from <a href="/wiki/Balliol_College,_Oxford" title="Balliol College, Oxford">Balliol College, Oxford</a>, or from pursuing any form of public career. His family's wealth enabled him to live a life of leisure while devoting himself to his literary and musical interests.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Although the musicologist <a href="/wiki/Watkins_Shaw" title="Watkins Shaw">Watkins Shaw</a> dismisses Jennens as "a conceited figure of no special ability",<sup id="cite_ref-Shaw11_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw11-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Donald_Burrows_(musicologist)" title="Donald Burrows (musicologist)">Donald Burrows</a> has written:  "of Jennens's musical literacy there can be no doubt". He was certainly devoted to Handel's music, having helped to finance the publication of every Handel score since <i><a href="/wiki/Rodelinda_(opera)" title="Rodelinda (opera)">Rodelinda</a></i> in 1725.<sup id="cite_ref-OMO2_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OMO2-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> By 1741, after their collaboration on <i>Saul</i>, a warm friendship had developed between the two, and Handel was a frequent visitor to the Jennens family estate at <a href="/wiki/Gopsall" title="Gopsall">Gopsall</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MT-Jennens_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MT-Jennens-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Jennens's letter to Holdsworth of 10 July 1741, in which he first mentions <i>Messiah</i>, suggests that the text was a recent work, probably assembled earlier that summer. As a devout <a href="/wiki/Anglican" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican">Anglican</a> and believer in scriptural authority, Jennens intended to challenge advocates of <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">Deism</a>, who rejected the doctrine of divine intervention in human affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-L76_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L76-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Shaw describes the text as "a meditation of our Lord as Messiah in Christian thought and belief", and despite his reservations on Jennens's character, concedes that the finished <a href="/wiki/Libretto" title="Libretto">wordbook</a> "amounts to little short of a work of genius".<sup id="cite_ref-Shaw11_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw11-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> There is no evidence that Handel played any active role in the selection or preparation of the text, such as he did in the case of <i>Saul</i>; it seems, rather, that he saw no need to make any significant amendment to Jennens's work.<sup id="cite_ref-B10_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B10-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">Composition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Composition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The music for <i>Messiah</i> was completed in 24 days of swift composition. Having received Jennens's text some time after 10 July 1741, Handel began work on it on 22 August. His records show that he had completed Part I in outline by 28 August, Part II by 6 September and Part III by 12 September, followed by two days of "filling up" to produce the finished work on 14 September. The <a href="/wiki/Autograph" title="Autograph">autograph</a> score's 259 pages show some signs of haste such as blots, scratchings-out, unfilled bars and other uncorrected errors, but according to the music scholar Richard Luckett the number of errors is remarkably small in a document of this length.<sup id="cite_ref-L86_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L86-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The original manuscript for <i>Messiah</i> is now held in the <a href="/wiki/British_Library" title="British Library">British Library</a>'s music collection.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> It is scored for 2 trumpets, timpani, 2 oboes, 2 violins, viola, and basso continuo (cello, double bass, and harpsichord).
</p><p>At the end of his manuscript Handel wrote the letters "SDG"—<i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Soli_Deo_Gloria" class="mw-redirect" title="Soli Deo Gloria">Soli Deo Gloria</a></i></i>, "To God alone the glory". This inscription, taken with the speed of composition, has encouraged belief in the apocryphal story that Handel wrote the music in a fervour of divine inspiration in which, as he wrote the "Hallelujah" chorus,  "he saw all heaven before him".<sup id="cite_ref-L86_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L86-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Burrows points out that many of Handel's operas, of comparable length and structure to <i>Messiah</i>, were composed within similar timescales between theatrical seasons. The effort of writing so much music in so short a time was not unusual for Handel and his contemporaries; Handel commenced his next oratorio, <i>Samson</i>, within a week of finishing <i>Messiah</i>, and completed his draft of this new work in a month.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> In accordance with his frequent practice when writing new works, Handel adapted existing compositions for use in <i>Messiah</i>, in this case drawing on two recently completed Italian duets and one written twenty years previously. Thus, <i><i lang="it" title="Italian language text">Se tu non-lasci amore</i></i> from 1722 became the basis of "O Death, where is thy sting?"; "His yoke is easy" and "And he shall purify" were drawn from <i><i lang="it" title="Italian language text">Quel fior che alla'ride</i></i> (July 1741), "Unto us a child is born" and "All we like sheep" from <i><i lang="it" title="Italian language text">Nò, di voi non-vo' fidarmi</i></i> (July 1741).<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-B61_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B61-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Handel's instrumentation in the score is often imprecise, again in line with contemporary convention, where the use of certain instruments and combinations was assumed and did not need to be written down by the composer; later copyists would fill in the details.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Before the first performance Handel made numerous revisions to his manuscript score, in part to match the forces available for the 1742 Dublin premiere; it is probable that his work was not performed as originally conceived in his lifetime.<sup id="cite_ref-B22_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B22-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Between 1742 and 1754 he continued to revise and recompose individual movements, sometimes to suit the requirements of particular singers.<sup id="cite_ref-B41_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B41-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The first published score of <i>Messiah</i> was issued in 1767, eight years after Handel's death, though this was based on relatively early manuscripts and included none of Handel's later revisions.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<h3><span id="Dublin.2C_1742"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Dublin,_1742">Dublin, 1742</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Dublin, 1742">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Musick-hall-dublin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Musick-hall-dublin.jpg/220px-Musick-hall-dublin.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="242" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Musick-hall-dublin.jpg/330px-Musick-hall-dublin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Musick-hall-dublin.jpg/440px-Musick-hall-dublin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="509" data-file-height="560" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Musick-hall-dublin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Great Music Hall in Fishamble Street, Dublin, where <i>Messiah</i> was first performed</div></div></div>
<p>Handel's decision to give a season of concerts in Dublin in the winter of 1741–42 arose from an invitation from the <a href="/wiki/William_Cavendish,_3rd_Duke_of_Devonshire" title="William Cavendish, 3rd Duke of Devonshire">Duke of Devonshire</a>, then serving as <a href="/wiki/Lord_Lieutenant_of_Ireland" title="Lord Lieutenant of Ireland">Lord Lieutenant of Ireland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Shaw24_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw24-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> A violinist friend of Handel's, <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Dubourg" title="Matthew Dubourg">Matthew Dubourg</a>, was in Dublin as the Lord Lieutenant's bandmaster; he would look after the tour's orchestral requirements.<sup id="cite_ref-Cole_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cole-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Whether Handel originally intended to perform <i>Messiah</i> in Dublin is uncertain; he did not inform Jennens of any such plan, for the latter wrote to Holdsworth on 2 December 1741: "...&#160;it was some mortification to me to hear that instead of performing <i>Messiah</i> here he has gone into Ireland with it."<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> After arriving in Dublin on 18 November 1741, Handel arranged a subscription series of six concerts, to be held between December 1741 and February 1742 at the Great Music Hall, <a href="/wiki/Fishamble_Street" title="Fishamble Street">Fishamble Street</a>. These concerts were so popular that a second series was quickly arranged; <i>Messiah</i> figured in neither series.<sup id="cite_ref-Shaw24_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw24-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In early March Handel began discussions with the appropriate committees for a charity concert, to be given in April, at which he intended to present <i>Messiah</i>. He sought and was given permission from <a href="/wiki/St_Patrick%27s_Cathedral,_Dublin" title="St Patrick&#39;s Cathedral, Dublin">St Patrick's</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christ_Church_Cathedral,_Dublin" title="Christ Church Cathedral, Dublin">Christ Church</a> cathedrals to use their choirs for this occasion.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-B17_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B17-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> These forces amounted to 16 men and 16 boy choristers; several of the men were allocated solo parts. The women soloists were <a href="/wiki/Christina_Maria_Avoglio" title="Christina Maria Avoglio">Christina Maria Avoglio</a>, who had sung the main soprano roles in the two subscription series, and <a href="/wiki/Susannah_Maria_Cibber" title="Susannah Maria Cibber">Susannah Cibber</a>, an established stage actress and <a href="/wiki/Contralto" title="Contralto">contralto</a> who had sung in the second series.<sup id="cite_ref-B17_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B17-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> To accommodate Cibber's vocal range, the recitative "Then shall the eyes of the blind" and the aria "He shall feed his flock" were transposed down to F major.<sup id="cite_ref-B22_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B22-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hog17_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hog17-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The performance, also in the Fishamble Street hall, was originally announced for 12 April, but was deferred for a day "at the request of persons of Distinction".<sup id="cite_ref-Shaw24_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw24-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The orchestra in Dublin comprised strings, two trumpets, and timpani; the number of players is unknown. Handel had his own organ shipped to Ireland for the performances; a harpsichord was probably also used.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The three charities that were to benefit were prisoners' debt relief, the Mercer's Hospital, and the Charitable Infirmary.<sup id="cite_ref-B17_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B17-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In its report on a public rehearsal, the <i>Dublin News-Letter</i> described the oratorio as "...&#160;far surpass[ing] anything of that Nature which has been performed in this or any other Kingdom".<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Seven hundred people attended the premiere on 13 April.<sup id="cite_ref-L127_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L127-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> So that the largest possible audience could be admitted to the concert, gentlemen were requested to remove their swords, and ladies were asked not to wear hoops in their dresses.<sup id="cite_ref-B17_41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B17-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The performance earned unanimous praise from the assembled press: "Words are wanting to express the exquisite delight it afforded to the admiring and crouded Audience".<sup id="cite_ref-L127_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L127-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> A Dublin clergyman, Rev. Delaney, was so overcome by Susanna Cibber's rendering of "He was despised" that reportedly he leapt to his feet and cried: "Woman, for this be all thy sins forgiven thee!"<sup id="cite_ref-Hog22_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hog22-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;n 3&#93;</a></sup> The takings amounted to around £400, providing about £127 to each of the three nominated charities and securing the release of 142 indebted prisoners.<sup id="cite_ref-Cole_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cole-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-L127_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L127-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Handel remained in Dublin for four months after the premiere. He organised a second performance of <i>Messiah</i> on 3 June, which was announced as "the last Performance of Mr Handel's during his Stay in this Kingdom". In this second <i>Messiah</i>, which was for Handel's private financial benefit, Cibber reprised her role from the first performance, though Avoglio may have been replaced by a Mrs Maclaine;<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> details of other performers are not recorded.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="London.2C_1743.E2.80.9359"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="London,_1743–59">London, 1743–59</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: London, 1743–59">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The warm reception accorded to <i>Messiah</i> in Dublin was not repeated in London when Handel introduced the work at <a href="/wiki/Royal_Opera_House#The_first_theatre" title="Royal Opera House">the Covent Garden theatre</a> on 23 March 1743. Avoglio and Cibber were again the chief soloists; they were joined by the tenor <a href="/wiki/John_Beard_(tenor)" title="John Beard (tenor)">John Beard</a>, a veteran of Handel's operas, the bass Thomas Rheinhold and two other sopranos, Kitty Clive and Miss Edwards.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The first performance was overshadowed by views expressed in the press that the work's subject matter was too exalted to be performed in a theatre, particularly by secular singer-actresses such as Cibber and Clive. In an attempt to deflect such sensibilities, in London Handel had avoided the name <i>Messiah</i> and presented the work as the "New Sacred Oratorio".<sup id="cite_ref-B24_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B24-53">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> As was his custom, Handel rearranged the music to suit his singers. He wrote a new setting of "And lo, the angel of the Lord" for Clive, never used subsequently. He added a tenor song for Beard: "Their sound is gone out", which had appeared in Jennens's original libretto but had not been in the Dublin performances.<sup id="cite_ref-B30_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B30-54">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The custom of standing for the "Hallelujah" chorus originates from a belief that, at the London premiere, King <a href="/wiki/George_II_of_Great_Britain" title="George II of Great Britain">George II</a> did so, which would have obliged all to stand. There is no convincing evidence that the king was present, or that he attended any subsequent performance of <i>Messiah</i>; the first reference to the practice of standing appears in a letter dated 1756, three years prior to Handel's death.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Microcosm_of_London_Plate_037_-_Foundling_Hospital.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Microcosm_of_London_Plate_037_-_Foundling_Hospital.jpg/220px-Microcosm_of_London_Plate_037_-_Foundling_Hospital.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Microcosm_of_London_Plate_037_-_Foundling_Hospital.jpg/330px-Microcosm_of_London_Plate_037_-_Foundling_Hospital.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Microcosm_of_London_Plate_037_-_Foundling_Hospital.jpg/440px-Microcosm_of_London_Plate_037_-_Foundling_Hospital.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1764" data-file-height="1338" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Microcosm_of_London_Plate_037_-_Foundling_Hospital.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The chapel of London's <a href="/wiki/Foundling_Hospital" title="Foundling Hospital">Foundling Hospital</a>, the venue for regular charity performances of <i>Messiah</i> from 1750</div></div></div>
<p>London's initially cool reception of <i>Messiah</i> led Handel to reduce the season's planned six performances to three, and not to present the work at all in 1744—to the considerable annoyance of Jennens, whose relations with the composer temporarily soured.<sup id="cite_ref-B24_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B24-53">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> At Jennens's request, Handel made several changes in the music for the 1745 revival: "Their sound is gone out" became a choral piece, the soprano song "Rejoice greatly" was recomposed in shortened form, and the transpositions for Cibber's voice were restored to their original soprano range.<sup id="cite_ref-B41_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B41-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Jennens wrote to Holdsworth on 30 August 1745: "[Handel] has made a fine Entertainment of it, though not near so good as he might &amp; ought to have done. I have with great difficulty made him correct some of the grosser faults in the composition&#160;..."  Handel directed two performances at Covent Garden in 1745, on 9 and 11 April,<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> and then set the work aside for four years.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The 1749 revival at Covent Garden, under the proper title of <i>Messiah</i>, saw the appearance of two female soloists who were henceforth closely associated with Handel's music: <a href="/wiki/Giulia_Frasi" title="Giulia Frasi">Giulia Frasi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caterina_Galli" title="Caterina Galli">Caterina Galli</a>. In the following year these were joined by the male alto <a href="/wiki/Gaetano_Guadagni" title="Gaetano Guadagni">Gaetano Guadagni</a>, for whom Handel composed new versions of "But who may abide" and "Thou art gone up on high". The year 1750 also saw the institution of the annual charity performances of <i>Messiah</i> at London's <a href="/wiki/Foundling_Hospital" title="Foundling Hospital">Foundling Hospital</a>, which continued until Handel's death and beyond.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The 1754 performance at the hospital is the first for which full details of the orchestral and vocal forces survive. The orchestra included fifteen violins, five violas, three cellos, two double-basses, four bassoons, four oboes, two trumpets, two horns and drums. In the chorus of nineteen were six trebles from the Chapel Royal; the remainder, all men, were altos, tenors and basses. Frasi, Galli and Beard led the five soloists, who were required to assist the chorus.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;n 4&#93;</a></sup> For this performance the transposed Guadagni arias were restored to the soprano voice.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> By 1754 Handel was severely afflicted by the onset of blindness, and in 1755 he turned over the direction of the <i>Messiah</i> hospital performance to his pupil, J.C. Smith.<sup id="cite_ref-Shaw51_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw51-64">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> He apparently resumed his duties in 1757 and may have continued thereafter.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The final performance of the work at which Handel was present was at Covent Garden on 6 April 1759, eight days before his death.<sup id="cite_ref-Shaw51_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw51-64">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_performance_history">Later performance history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Later performance history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="18th_century">18th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 18th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Messiah-Westminster-Abbey-1787.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Messiah-Westminster-Abbey-1787.jpg/220px-Messiah-Westminster-Abbey-1787.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="365" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Messiah-Westminster-Abbey-1787.jpg/330px-Messiah-Westminster-Abbey-1787.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Messiah-Westminster-Abbey-1787.jpg/440px-Messiah-Westminster-Abbey-1787.jpg 2x" data-file-width="698" data-file-height="1158" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Messiah-Westminster-Abbey-1787.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1787 advertisement for <i>Messiah</i> at <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> with 800 performers</div></div></div>
<p>During the 1750s  <i>Messiah</i> was performed increasingly at festivals and cathedrals throughout the country.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Individual choruses and arias were occasionally extracted for use as anthems or <a href="/wiki/Motet" title="Motet">motets</a> in church services, or as concert pieces, a practice that grew in the 19th century and has continued ever since.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> After Handel's death, performances were given in <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a> (1768), New York (excerpts, 1770), <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a> (1772), and <a href="/wiki/Mannheim" title="Mannheim">Mannheim</a> (1777), where <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Mozart</a> first heard it.<sup id="cite_ref-chron_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chron-68">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> For the performances in Handel's lifetime and in the decades following his death, the musical forces used in the Foundling Hospital performance of 1754 are thought by Burrows to be typical.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> A fashion for large-scale performances began in 1784, in a series of commemorative concerts of Handel's music given in <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> under the patronage of <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">King George III</a>. A plaque on the Abbey wall records that "The Band consisting of DXXV [525] vocal &amp; instrumental performers was conducted by Joah Bates Esqr."<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> In a 1955 article, <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_Sargent" title="Malcolm Sargent">Sir Malcolm Sargent</a>, a proponent of large-scale performances, wrote, "Mr Bates&#160;... had known Handel well and respected his wishes. The orchestra employed was two hundred and fifty strong, including twelve horns, twelve trumpets, six trombones and three pairs of timpani (some made especially large)."<sup id="cite_ref-sargent_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sargent-71">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In 1787 further performances were given at the Abbey; advertisements promised, "The Band will consist of Eight Hundred Performers".<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In continental Europe, performances of <i>Messiah</i> were departing from Handel's practices in a different way: his score was being drastically reorchestrated to suit contemporary tastes. In 1786, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Adam_Hiller" title="Johann Adam Hiller">Johann Adam Hiller</a> presented <i>Messiah</i> with updated scoring in <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Cathedral" title="Berlin Cathedral">Berlin Cathedral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-hiller_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hiller-73">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In 1788 Hiller presented a performance of his revision with a choir of 259 and an orchestra of 87 strings, 10 bassoons, 11 oboes, 8 flutes, 8 horns, 4 clarinets, 4 trombones, 7 trumpets, timpani, harpsichord and organ.<sup id="cite_ref-hiller_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hiller-73">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In 1789, Mozart was commissioned by Baron <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_van_Swieten" title="Gottfried van Swieten">Gottfried van Swieten</a> and the <i lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Gesellschaft_der_Associierten" title="Gesellschaft der Associierten">Gesellschaft der Associierten</a></i> to re-orchestrate several works by Handel, including  <i>Messiah</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Der_Messias" title="Der Messias">Der Messias</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;n 5&#93;</a></sup> Writing for a small-scale performance, he eliminated the organ continuo, added parts for flutes, clarinets, trombones and horns, recomposed some passages and rearranged others. The performance took place on 6 March 1789 in the rooms of Count Johann Esterházy, with four soloists and a choir of 12.<sup id="cite_ref-rl338_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rl338-77">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;n 6&#93;</a></sup> Mozart's arrangement, with minor amendments from Hiller, was published in 1803, after his death.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;n 7&#93;</a></sup> The musical scholar <a href="/wiki/Moritz_Hauptmann" title="Moritz Hauptmann">Moritz Hauptmann</a> described the Mozart additions as "<a href="/wiki/Stucco" title="Stucco">stucco</a> ornaments on a marble temple".<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Mozart himself was reportedly circumspect about his changes, insisting that any alterations to Handel's score should not be interpreted as an effort to improve the music.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Elements of this version later became familiar to British audiences, incorporated into editions of the score by editors including <a href="/wiki/Ebenezer_Prout" title="Ebenezer Prout">Ebenezer Prout</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rl338_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rl338-77">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Crystal-palace-handel-1857.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f1/Crystal-palace-handel-1857.jpg/240px-Crystal-palace-handel-1857.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f1/Crystal-palace-handel-1857.jpg/360px-Crystal-palace-handel-1857.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f1/Crystal-palace-handel-1857.jpg/480px-Crystal-palace-handel-1857.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="491" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Crystal-palace-handel-1857.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Handel Festival at <a href="/wiki/The_Crystal_Palace" title="The Crystal Palace">The Crystal Palace</a>, 1857</div></div></div>
<p>In the 19th century, approaches to Handel in German and English-speaking countries diverged further. In Leipzig in 1856, the musicologist <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Chrysander" title="Friedrich Chrysander">Friedrich Chrysander</a> and the literary historian <a href="/wiki/Georg_Gottfried_Gervinus" title="Georg Gottfried Gervinus">Georg Gottfried Gervinus</a> founded the Deutsche <a href="/wiki/H%C3%A4ndel-Gesellschaft" title="Händel-Gesellschaft">Händel-Gesellschaft</a> with the aim of publishing authentic editions of all Handel's works.<sup id="cite_ref-chron_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chron-68">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> At the same time, performances in Britain and the United States moved away from Handel's performance practice with increasingly grandiose renditions. <i>Messiah</i> was presented in New York in 1853 with a chorus of 300 and in Boston in 1865 with more than 600.
<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> In Britain a "Great Handel Festival" was held at <a href="/wiki/The_Crystal_Palace" title="The Crystal Palace">the Crystal Palace</a> in 1857, performing <i>Messiah</i> and other Handel oratorios, with a chorus of 2,000 singers and an orchestra of 500.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1860s and 1870s ever larger forces were assembled. <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">Bernard Shaw</a>, in his role as a music critic, commented, "The stale wonderment which the great chorus never fails to elicit has already been exhausted";<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> he later wrote, "Why, instead of wasting huge sums on the multitudinous dullness of a Handel Festival does not somebody set up a thoroughly rehearsed and exhaustively studied performance of the Messiah in <a href="/wiki/St_James%27s_Hall" title="St James&#39;s Hall">St James's Hall</a> with a chorus of twenty capable artists? Most of us would be glad to hear the work seriously performed once before we die."<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The employment of huge forces necessitated considerable augmentation of the orchestral parts. Many admirers of Handel believed that the composer would have made such additions, had the appropriate instruments been available in his day.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Shaw argued, largely unheeded, that "the composer may be spared from his friends, and the function of writing or selecting 'additional orchestral accompaniments' exercised with due discretion."<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One reason for the popularity of huge-scale performances was the ubiquity of amateur choral societies. The conductor <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Beecham" title="Thomas Beecham">Sir Thomas Beecham</a> wrote that for 200 years the chorus was "the national medium of musical utterance" in Britain. However, after the heyday of Victorian choral societies, he noted a "rapid and violent reaction against monumental performances&#160;... an appeal from several quarters that Handel should be played and heard as in the days between 1700 and 1750".<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> At the end of the century, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Bridge" title="Frederick Bridge">Sir Frederick Bridge</a> and T. W. Bourne pioneered revivals of <i>Messiah</i> in Handel's orchestration, and Bourne's work was the basis for further scholarly versions in the early 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century_and_beyond">20th century and beyond</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 20th century and beyond">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ebenezer-prout.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Ebenezer-prout.jpg/170px-Ebenezer-prout.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="269" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Ebenezer-prout.jpg/255px-Ebenezer-prout.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/dd/Ebenezer-prout.jpg 2x" data-file-width="293" data-file-height="463" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ebenezer-prout.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ebenezer_Prout" title="Ebenezer Prout">Ebenezer Prout</a> in 1899</div></div></div>
<p>Although the huge-scale oratorio tradition was perpetuated by such large ensembles as the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Choral_Society" title="Royal Choral Society">Royal Choral Society</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mormon_Tabernacle_Choir" title="Mormon Tabernacle Choir">Mormon Tabernacle Choir</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Huddersfield_Choral_Society" title="Huddersfield Choral Society">Huddersfield Choral Society</a> in the 20th century,<sup id="cite_ref-blyth_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blyth-95">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> there were increasing calls for performances more faithful to Handel's conception. At the turn of the century, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Musical_Times" title="The Musical Times">The Musical Times</a></i> wrote of the "additional accompaniments" of Mozart and others, "Is it not time that some of these 'hangers on' of Handel's score were sent about their business?"<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> In 1902, the musicologist Ebenezer Prout produced a new edition of the score, working from Handel's original manuscripts rather than from corrupt printed versions with errors accumulated from one edition to another.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;n 8&#93;</a></sup> However, Prout started from the assumption that a faithful reproduction of Handel's original score would not be practical:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>[T]he attempts made from time to time by our musical societies to give Handel's music as he meant it to be given must, however earnest the intention, and however careful the preparation, be foredoomed to failure from the very nature of the case. With our large choral societies, additional accompaniments of some kind are a necessity for an effective performance; and the question is not so much whether, as how they are to be written.<sup id="cite_ref-prout1_81-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prout1-81">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Prout continued the practice of adding flutes, clarinets and trombones to Handel's orchestration, but he restored Handel's high trumpet parts, which Mozart had omitted (evidently because playing them was a lost art by 1789).<sup id="cite_ref-prout1_81-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prout1-81">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> There was little dissent from Prout's approach, and when Chrysander's scholarly edition was published in the same year, it was received respectfully as "a volume for the study" rather than a performing edition, being an edited reproduction of various of Handel's manuscript versions.<sup id="cite_ref-cummings_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cummings-98">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> An authentic performance was thought impossible: <i>The Musical Times</i> correspondent wrote, "Handel's orchestral instruments were all (excepting the trumpet) of a coarser quality than those at present in use; his harpsichords are gone for ever&#160;... the places in which he performed the 'Messiah' were mere drawing-rooms when compared with the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Albert_Hall" title="Royal Albert Hall">Albert Hall</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_Hall" title="Queen&#39;s Hall">Queen's Hall</a> and the Crystal Palace.<sup id="cite_ref-cummings_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cummings-98">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> In Australia, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Register_(Adelaide)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Register (Adelaide)">The Register</a></i> protested at the prospect of performances by "trumpery little church choirs of 20 voices or so".<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Germany, <i>Messiah</i> was not so often performed as in Britain;<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> when it was given, medium-sized forces were the norm. At the <a href="/wiki/Handel_Festival,_Halle" title="Handel Festival, Halle">Handel Festival</a> held in 1922 in Handel's native town, Halle, his choral works were given by a choir of 163 and an orchestra of 64.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> In Britain, innovative broadcasting and recording contributed to reconsideration of Handelian performance. For example, in 1928, Beecham conducted a recording of <i>Messiah</i> with modestly sized forces and controversially brisk tempi, although the orchestration remained far from authentic.<sup id="cite_ref-klein_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-klein-102">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> In 1934 and 1935, the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> broadcast performances of <i>Messiah</i> conducted by <a href="/wiki/Adrian_Boult" title="Adrian Boult">Adrian Boult</a> with "a faithful adherence to Handel's clear scoring."<sup id="cite_ref-dickinson_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dickinson-103">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> A performance with authentic scoring was given in <a href="/wiki/Worcester_Cathedral" title="Worcester Cathedral">Worcester Cathedral</a> as part of the <a href="/wiki/Three_Choirs_Festival" title="Three Choirs Festival">Three Choirs Festival</a> in 1935.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> In 1950 John Tobin conducted a performance of <i>Messiah</i> in <a href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_Cathedral" title="St Paul&#39;s Cathedral">St Paul's Cathedral</a> with the orchestral forces specified by the composer, a choir of 60, a <a href="/wiki/Counter-tenor" class="mw-redirect" title="Counter-tenor">counter-tenor</a> alto soloist, and modest attempts at vocal elaboration of the printed notes, in the manner of Handel's day.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The Prout version sung with many voices remained popular with British choral societies, but at the same time increasingly frequent performances were given by small professional ensembles in suitably sized venues, using authentic scoring. Recordings on LP and CD were preponderantly of the latter type, and the large scale <i>Messiah</i> came to seem old-fashioned.<sup id="cite_ref-which_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-which-106">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ENO_Messiah_2009_Crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/ENO_Messiah_2009_Crop.jpg/300px-ENO_Messiah_2009_Crop.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/ENO_Messiah_2009_Crop.jpg/450px-ENO_Messiah_2009_Crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ee/ENO_Messiah_2009_Crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="552" data-file-height="289" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ENO_Messiah_2009_Crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Messiah</i> staged at the <a href="/wiki/English_National_Opera" title="English National Opera">English National Opera</a>, 2009</div></div></div>
<p>The cause of authentic performance was advanced in 1965 by the publication of a new edition of the score, edited by <a href="/wiki/Watkins_Shaw" title="Watkins Shaw">Watkins Shaw</a>. In the <i><a href="/wiki/Grove_Dictionary_of_Music_and_Musicians" class="mw-redirect" title="Grove Dictionary of Music and Musicians">Grove Dictionary of Music and Musicians</a></i>, David Scott writes, "the edition at first aroused suspicion on account of its attempts in several directions to break the crust of convention surrounding the work in the British Isles."<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> By the time of Shaw's death in 1996, <i>The Times</i> described his edition as "now in universal use".<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;n 9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Messiah</i> remains Handel's best-known work, with performances particularly popular during the Advent season;<sup id="cite_ref-Smithsonian_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smithsonian-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> writing in December 1993, the music critic <a href="/wiki/Alex_Ross_(music_critic)" title="Alex Ross (music critic)">Alex Ross</a> refers to that month's 21 performances in New York alone as "numbing repetition".<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Against the general trend towards authenticity, the work has been staged in opera houses, both in London (2009) and in Paris (2011).<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The Mozart score is revived from time to time,<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> and in <a href="/wiki/Anglophone" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglophone">Anglophone</a> countries "<a href="/wiki/Scratch_Messiah" title="Scratch Messiah">singalong</a>" performances with many hundreds of performers are popular.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Although performances striving for authenticity are now usual, it is generally agreed that there can never be a definitive version of <i>Messiah</i>; the surviving manuscripts contain radically different settings of many numbers, and vocal and instrumental ornamentation of the written notes is a matter of personal judgment, even for the most historically informed performers.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The Handel scholar <a href="/wiki/Winton_Dean" title="Winton Dean">Winton Dean</a> has written:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>[T]here is still plenty for scholars to fight over, and more than ever for conductors to decide for themselves. Indeed if they are not prepared to grapple with the problems presented by the score they ought not to conduct it. This applies not only to the choice of versions, but to every aspect of baroque practice, and of course there are often no final answers.<sup id="cite_ref-dean_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dean-109">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organisation_and_numbering_of_movements">Organisation and numbering of movements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Organisation and numbering of movements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Structure_of_Handel%27s_Messiah" title="Structure of Handel&#39;s Messiah">Structure of Handel's Messiah</a></div>
<p>The numbering of the movements shown here is in accordance with the Novello vocal score (1959), edited by Watkins Shaw, which adapts the numbering earlier devised by Ebenezer Prout. Other editions count the movements slightly differently; the <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A4renreiter" title="Bärenreiter">Bärenreiter</a> edition of 1965, for example, does not number all the <a href="/wiki/Recitative" title="Recitative">recitatives</a> and runs from 1 to 47.<sup id="cite_ref-Bix_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bix-116">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> The division into parts and scenes is based on the 1743 word-book prepared for the first London performance.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The scene headings are given as Burrows summarised the <a href="/wiki/Messiah_structure#Scenes" class="mw-redirect" title="Messiah structure">scene headings</a> by Jennens.<sup id="cite_ref-B57_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B57-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div>

&#32;
