<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hallelujah_score_1741.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Hallelujah_score_1741.jpg/220px-Hallelujah_score_1741.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Hallelujah_score_1741.jpg/330px-Hallelujah_score_1741.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Hallelujah_score_1741.jpg/440px-Hallelujah_score_1741.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1646" data-file-height="1280" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hallelujah_score_1741.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The final bars of the "Hallelujah" chorus, from Handel's manuscript</div></div></div>
<p>Handel's music for <i>Messiah</i>  is distinguished from most of his other oratorios by an orchestral restraint—a quality which the musicologist <a href="/wiki/Percy_M._Young" title="Percy M. Young">Percy M. Young</a> observes was not adopted by Mozart and other later arrangers of the music.<sup id="cite_ref-Young63_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Young63-118">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> The work begins quietly, with instrumental and solo movements preceding the first appearance of the chorus, whose entry in the low alto register is muted.<sup id="cite_ref-Hog17_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hog17-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> A particular aspect of Handel's restraint is his limited use of trumpets throughout the work. After their introduction in the Part I chorus "Glory to God", apart from the solo in "The trumpet shall sound" they are heard only in "Hallelujah" and the final chorus "Worthy is the Lamb".<sup id="cite_ref-Young63_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Young63-118">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> It is this rarity, says Young, that makes these brass interpolations particularly effective: "Increase them and the thrill is diminished".<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> In  "Glory to God", Handel marked the entry of the trumpets as <i>da lontano e un poco piano</i>, meaning "quietly, from afar"; his original intention had been to place the brass offstage (<i>in disparte</i>) at this point, to highlight the effect of distance.<sup id="cite_ref-B61_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B61-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-L93_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L93-120">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>  In this initial appearance the trumpets lack the expected drum accompaniment, "a deliberate withholding of effect, leaving something in reserve for Parts II and III"  according to Luckett.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although <i>Messiah</i> is not in any particular key, Handel's tonal scheme has been summarised by the musicologist <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Hicks" title="Anthony Hicks">Anthony Hicks</a> as "an aspiration towards D major", the key musically associated with light and glory. As the oratorio moves forward with various shifts in key to reflect changes in mood, D major emerges at significant points, primarily the "trumpet" movements with their uplifting messages. It is the key in which the work reaches its triumphant ending.<sup id="cite_ref-H10_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H10-122">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> In the absence of a predominant key, other integrating elements have been proposed. For example, the musicologist Rudolf Steglich has suggested that Handel used the device of the "ascending <a href="/wiki/Perfect_fourth" title="Perfect fourth">fourth</a>"  as a unifying <a href="/wiki/Motif_(music)" title="Motif (music)">motif</a>; this device most noticeably occurs in the first two notes of "I know that my Redeemer liveth" and on numerous other occasions. Nevertheless, Luckett finds this thesis implausible, and asserts that "the unity of <i>Messiah</i> is a consequence of nothing more arcane than the quality of Handel's attention to his text, and the consistency of his musical imagination".<sup id="cite_ref-L88_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L88-123">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Allan_Kozinn" title="Allan Kozinn">Allan Kozinn</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> music critic, finds "a model marriage of music and text&#160;... From the gentle falling melody assigned to the opening words ("Comfort ye") to the sheer ebullience of the "Hallelujah" chorus and the ornate celebratory counterpoint that supports the closing "Amen", hardly a line of text goes by that Handel does not amplify".<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Part_I">Part I</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Part I">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Messiah_Part_I" title="Messiah Part I">Messiah Part I</a></div>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small noprint" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Handel_-_messiah_-_02_comfort_ye.ogg" title="File:Handel - messiah - 02 comfort ye.ogg"><b>No. 2.</b> Recit. accompanied (<i>Tenor</i>): <i>Comfort ye my people</i></a></div>
<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="171.10204081633" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Handel_-_messiah_-_02_comfort_ye.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6b/Handel_-_messiah_-_02_comfort_ye.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (116 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="116039" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/6/6b/Handel_-_messiah_-_02_comfort_ye.ogg/Handel_-_messiah_-_02_comfort_ye.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="202672" /><track src="https://commons.wikimedia.org/w/api.php?action=timedtext&amp;title=File%3AHandel_-_messiah_-_02_comfort_ye.ogg&amp;lang=en&amp;trackformat=srt&amp;origin=%2A" kind="subtitles" type="text/x-srt" srclang="en" label="English (en) subtitles" data-dir="ltr" /></audio></div></div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0"></div></div></td></tr>
<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>The opening  <i>Sinfony</i> is composed in E minor for strings, and is Handel's first use in oratorio of the <a href="/wiki/French_overture" title="French overture">French overture</a> form. Jennens commented that the <i>Sinfony</i> contains "passages far unworthy of Handel, but much more unworthy of the Messiah";<sup id="cite_ref-L88_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L88-123">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Handel's early biographer <a href="/wiki/Charles_Burney" title="Charles Burney">Charles Burney</a> merely found it "dry and uninteresting".<sup id="cite_ref-Hog17_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hog17-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> A change of key to E major leads to the first prophecy, delivered by the tenor whose vocal line in the opening recitative "Comfort ye" is entirely independent of the strings accompaniment. The music proceeds through various key changes as the prophecies unfold, culminating in the G major chorus "For unto us a child is born", in which the choral exclamations (which include an ascending fourth in "the Mighty God") are imposed on material drawn from Handel's Italian cantata <i>Nò, di voi non-vo'fidarmi</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hog17_43-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hog17-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Such passages, says the music historian <a href="/wiki/Donald_Jay_Grout" title="Donald Jay Grout">Donald Jay Grout</a>, "reveal Handel the dramatist, the unerring master of dramatic effect".<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The pastoral interlude that follows begins with the short instrumental movement, the <i>Pifa</i>, which takes its name from the shepherd-bagpipers, or <i>pifferari</i>, who played their pipes in the streets of Rome at Christmas time.<sup id="cite_ref-L93_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L93-120">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Handel wrote the movement in both 11-bar and extended 32-bar forms; according to Burrows, either will work in performance.<sup id="cite_ref-B41_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B41-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The group of four short recitatives which follow it introduce the soprano soloist—although often the earlier aria "But who may abide" is sung by the soprano in its transposed G minor form.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> The final recitative of this section is in D major and heralds the affirmative chorus "Glory to God". The remainder of Part I is largely carried by the soprano in B flat, in what Burrows terms a rare instance of tonal stability.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The aria "He shall feed his flock" underwent several transformations by Handel, appearing at different times as a recitative, an alto aria and a duet for alto and soprano before the original soprano version was restored in 1754.<sup id="cite_ref-Hog17_43-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hog17-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The appropriateness of the Italian source material for the setting of the solemn concluding chorus "His yoke is easy" has been questioned by the music scholar <a href="/wiki/Sedley_Taylor" title="Sedley Taylor">Sedley Taylor</a>, who calls it "a piece of word-painting&#160;... grievously out of place", though he concedes that the four-part choral conclusion is a stroke of genius that combines beauty with dignity.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Part_II">Part II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Part II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Messiah_Part_II" title="Messiah Part II">Messiah Part II</a></div>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small noprint" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Handel_-_messiah_-_23_he_was_despised.ogg" title="File:Handel - messiah - 23 he was despised.ogg"><b>No. 23.</b> Air (<i>Alto</i>): <i>He was despised</i></a></div>
<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_1" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="526.88979591837" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Handel_-_messiah_-_23_he_was_despised.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d3/Handel_-_messiah_-_23_he_was_despised.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (114 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="113998" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/d3/Handel_-_messiah_-_23_he_was_despised.ogg/Handel_-_messiah_-_23_he_was_despised.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="197712" /></audio></div></div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0"></div></div></td></tr>
<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>The second Part begins in G minor, a key which, in Hogwood's phrase, brings a mood of "tragic presentiment" to the long sequence of Passion numbers which follows.<sup id="cite_ref-Hog22_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hog22-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The declamatory opening chorus "Behold the Lamb of God", in <a href="/wiki/Fugue" title="Fugue">fugal</a> form, is followed by the alto solo "He was despised" in E flat major, the longest single item in the oratorio, in which some phrases are sung unaccompanied to emphasise Christ's abandonment.<sup id="cite_ref-Hog22_47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hog22-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Luckett records Burney's description of this number as "the highest idea of excellence in pathetic expression of any English song".<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> The subsequent series of mainly short choral movements cover Christ's Passion, Crucifixion, Death and Resurrection, at first in F minor, with a brief F major respite in "All we like sheep". Here, Handel's use of <i>Nò, di voi non-vo'fidarmi</i> has Sedley Taylor's unqualified approval: "[Handel] bids the voices enter in solemn canonical sequence, and his chorus ends with a combination of grandeur and depth of feeling such as is at the command of consummate genius only".<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small noprint" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Handel_-_messiah_-_44_hallelujah.ogg" title="File:Handel - messiah - 44 hallelujah.ogg"><b>No. 44.</b> Chorus: <i>Hallelujah</i></a></div>
<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_2" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="227.38553287982" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Handel_-_messiah_-_44_hallelujah.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/ce/Handel_-_messiah_-_44_hallelujah.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (119 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="119201" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/c/ce/Handel_-_messiah_-_44_hallelujah.ogg/Handel_-_messiah_-_44_hallelujah.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="211168" /></audio></div></div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0"></div></div></td></tr>
<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>The sense of desolation returns, in what Hogwood calls the "remote and barbarous" key of B flat minor, for the tenor recitative "All they that see him".<sup id="cite_ref-Hog22_47-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hog22-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> The sombre sequence finally ends with the Ascension chorus "Lift up your heads", which Handel initially divides between two choral groups, the altos serving both as the bass line to a soprano choir and the treble line to the tenors and basses.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> For the 1754 Foundling Hospital performance Handel added two horns, which join in when the chorus unites towards the end of the number.<sup id="cite_ref-Hog22_47-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hog22-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> After the celebratory tone of Christ's reception into heaven, marked by the choir's D major acclamation "Let all the angels of God worship him", the "<a href="/wiki/Pentecost" title="Pentecost">Whitsun</a>" section proceeds through a series of contrasting moods—serene and pastoral in "How beautiful are the feet", theatrically operatic in "Why do the nations so furiously rage"—towards the Part II culmination of "Hallelujah". This, as Young points out, is not the climactic chorus of the work, although one cannot escape its "contagious enthusiasm".<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> It builds from a deceptively light orchestral opening,<sup id="cite_ref-Hog22_47-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hog22-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> through a short, unison <a href="/wiki/Cantus_firmus" title="Cantus firmus">cantus firmus</a> passage on the words "For the Lord God omnipotent reigneth", to the reappearance of the long-silent trumpets at "And He shall reign for ever and ever". Commentators have noted that the musical line for this third subject is based on <i><a href="/wiki/Wachet_auf,_ruft_uns_die_Stimme" title="Wachet auf, ruft uns die Stimme">Wachet auf</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Philipp_Nicolai" title="Philipp Nicolai">Philipp Nicolai</a>'s popular <a href="/wiki/Lutheran_chorale" title="Lutheran chorale">Lutheran chorale</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hog22_47-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hog22-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Part_III">Part III</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Part III">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Messiah_Part_III" title="Messiah Part III">Messiah Part III</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Worthy-is-the-lamb.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Worthy-is-the-lamb.jpg/220px-Worthy-is-the-lamb.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Worthy-is-the-lamb.jpg/330px-Worthy-is-the-lamb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Worthy-is-the-lamb.jpg/440px-Worthy-is-the-lamb.jpg 2x" data-file-width="545" data-file-height="444" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Worthy-is-the-lamb.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First page of the concluding chorus "Worthy is the Lamb": From Handel's original manuscript in the <a href="/wiki/British_Library" title="British Library">British Library</a>, London.</div></div></div>
<p>The opening soprano solo in E major, "I know that my Redeemer liveth" is one of the few numbers in the oratorio that has remained unrevised from its original form.<sup id="cite_ref-H26_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H26-135">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Its simple unison violin accompaniment and its consoling rhythms apparently brought tears to Burney's eyes.<sup id="cite_ref-L104_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L104-136">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> It is followed by a quiet chorus that leads to the bass's declamation in D major: "Behold, I tell you a mystery", then the long aria "The trumpet shall sound", marked <i><i lang="it" title="Italian language text">pomposo ma non-allegro</i></i> ("dignified but not fast").<sup id="cite_ref-H26_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H26-135">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Handel originally wrote this in <a href="/wiki/Da_capo" title="Da capo">da capo</a> form, but shortened it to <a href="/wiki/Dal_segno" title="Dal segno">dal segno</a>, probably before the first performance.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> The extended, characteristic trumpet tune that precedes and accompanies the voice is the only significant instrumental solo in the entire oratorio. Handel's awkward, repeated stressing of the fourth syllable of "incorruptible" may have been the source of the 18th-century poet <a href="/wiki/William_Shenstone" title="William Shenstone">William Shenstone</a>'s comment that he "could observe some parts in <i><a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">Messiah</a></i> wherein Handel's judgements failed him; where the music was not equal, or was even <i>opposite</i>, to what the words required".<sup id="cite_ref-H26_135-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H26-135">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> After a brief solo recitative, the alto is joined by the tenor for the only duet in Handel's final version of the music, "O death, where is thy sting?"  The melody is adapted from Handel's 1722 cantata <i><i lang="it" title="Italian language text">Se tu non-lasci amore</i></i>, and is in Luckett's view the most successful of the Italian borrowings.<sup id="cite_ref-L104_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L104-136">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The duet runs straight into the chorus "But thanks be to God".<sup id="cite_ref-H26_135-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H26-135">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The reflective soprano solo "If God be for us" (originally written for alto) quotes <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Luther</a>'s chorale <i><i lang="de" title="German language text"><a href="/wiki/Aus_tiefer_Not_schrei_ich_zu_dir" title="Aus tiefer Not schrei ich zu dir">Aus tiefer Not</a></i></i>. It ushers in the D major choral finale: "Worthy is the Lamb", leading to the apocalyptic "Amen" in which, says Hogwood, "the entry of the trumpets marks the final storming of heaven".<sup id="cite_ref-H26_135-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H26-135">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Handel's first biographer, <a href="/wiki/John_Mainwaring" title="John Mainwaring">John Mainwaring</a>, wrote in 1760 that this conclusion revealed the composer "rising still higher" than in "that vast effort of genius, the Hallelujah chorus".<sup id="cite_ref-L104_136-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L104-136">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> Young writes that the "Amen"  should, in the manner of <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Pierluigi_da_Palestrina" title="Giovanni Pierluigi da Palestrina">Palestrina</a>, "be delivered as though through the aisles and ambulatories of some great church".<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recordings">Recordings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Recordings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Many early recordings of individual choruses and arias from <i>Messiah</i> reflect the performance styles then fashionable—large forces, slow tempi and liberal reorchestration. Typical examples are choruses conducted by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Wood" title="Henry Wood">Sir Henry Wood</a>, recorded in 1926 for <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Graphophone_Company" title="Columbia Graphophone Company">Columbia</a> with the 3,500-strong choir and orchestra of the Crystal Palace Handel Festival, and a contemporary rival disc from <a href="/wiki/HMV_Records" class="mw-redirect" title="HMV Records">HMV</a> featuring the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Choral_Society" title="Royal Choral Society">Royal Choral Society</a> under Malcolm Sargent, recorded at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Albert_Hall" title="Royal Albert Hall">Royal Albert Hall</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first near-complete recording of the whole work (with the cuts then customary)<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;n 10&#93;</a></sup> was conducted by Sir Thomas Beecham in 1928. It represented an effort by Beecham to "provide an interpretation which, in his opinion, was nearer the composer's intentions", with smaller forces and faster tempi than had become traditional.<sup id="cite_ref-klein_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-klein-102">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> His contralto soloist, <a href="/wiki/Muriel_Brunskill" title="Muriel Brunskill">Muriel Brunskill</a>, later commented, "His tempi, which are now taken for granted, were revolutionary; he entirely revitalised it".<sup id="cite_ref-blyth_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blyth-95">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, Sargent retained the large scale tradition in his four HMV recordings, the first in 1946 and three more in the 1950s and 1960s, all with the <a href="/wiki/Huddersfield_Choral_Society" title="Huddersfield Choral Society">Huddersfield Choral Society</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Liverpool_Philharmonic" title="Royal Liverpool Philharmonic">Liverpool Philharmonic Orchestra</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-blyth_95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blyth-95">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Beecham's second recording of the work, in 1947, "led the way towards more truly Handelian rhythms and speeds", according to the critic <a href="/wiki/Alan_Blyth" title="Alan Blyth">Alan Blyth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-blyth_95-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blyth-95">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> In a 1991 study of all 76 complete <i>Messiahs</i> recorded by that date, the writer Teri Noel Towe called this version of Beecham's "one of a handful of truly stellar performances".<sup id="cite_ref-blyth_95-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blyth-95">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1954 the first recording based on Handel's original scoring was conducted by <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Scherchen" title="Hermann Scherchen">Hermann Scherchen</a> for <a href="/wiki/Nixa_Records" title="Nixa Records">Nixa</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;n 11&#93;</a></sup> quickly followed by a version, judged scholarly at the time, under Sir Adrian Boult for <a href="/wiki/Decca_Records" title="Decca Records">Decca</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rg_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rg-141">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> By the standards of 21st-century performance, however, Scherchen's and Boult's tempi were still slow, and there was no attempt at vocal ornamentation by the soloists.<sup id="cite_ref-rg_141-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rg-141">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> In 1966 and 1967 two new recordings were regarded as great advances in scholarship and performance practice, conducted respectively by <a href="/wiki/Colin_Davis" title="Colin Davis">Colin Davis</a> for <a href="/wiki/Philips_Records" title="Philips Records">Philips</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Mackerras" title="Charles Mackerras">Charles Mackerras</a> for HMV. They inaugurated a new tradition of brisk, small scale performances, with vocal embellishments by the solo singers.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;n 12&#93;</a></sup>    Among recordings of older-style performances are Beecham's 1959 recording with the Royal Philharmonic Orchestra, with orchestration commissioned from Sir Eugene Goossens and completed by the English composer <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Salzedo" title="Leonard Salzedo">Leonard Salzedo</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-blyth_95-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blyth-95">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Karl_Richter_(conductor)" title="Karl Richter (conductor)">Karl Richter</a>'s 1973 version for <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Grammophon" title="Deutsche Grammophon">DG</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/David_Willcocks" title="David Willcocks">David Willcocks</a>'s 1995 performance based on Prout's 1902 edition of the score, with a 325-voice choir and 90-piece orchestra.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By the end of the 1970s the quest for authenticity had extended to the use of period instruments and historically correct styles of playing them. The first of such versions were conducted by the early music specialists <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Hogwood" title="Christopher Hogwood">Christopher Hogwood</a> (1979) and <a href="/wiki/John_Eliot_Gardiner" title="John Eliot Gardiner">John Eliot Gardiner</a> (1982).<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> The use of period instruments quickly became the norm on record, although some conductors, among them <a href="/wiki/Georg_Solti" title="Georg Solti">Sir Georg Solti</a> (1985), continued to favour modern instruments. <i><a href="/wiki/Gramophone_(magazine)" title="Gramophone (magazine)">Gramophone</a></i> magazine and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Penguin_Guide_to_Recorded_Classical_Music" title="The Penguin Guide to Recorded Classical Music">The Penguin Guide to Recorded Classical Music</a></i> highlighted two versions, conducted respectively by <a href="/wiki/Trevor_Pinnock" title="Trevor Pinnock">Trevor Pinnock</a> (1988) and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Hickox" title="Richard Hickox">Richard Hickox</a> (1992). The latter employs a chorus of 24 singers and an orchestra of 31 players; Handel is known to have used a chorus of 19 and an orchestra of 37.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Performances on an even smaller scale have followed.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;n 13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Several reconstructions of early performances have been recorded: the 1742 Dublin version by Scherchen in 1954, and again in 1959, and by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Claude_Malgoire" title="Jean-Claude Malgoire">Jean-Claude Malgoire</a> in 1980<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> There are several recordings of the 1754 Foundling Hospital version, including those under Hogwood (1979), <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Parrott" title="Andrew Parrott">Andrew Parrott</a> (1989), and Paul McCreesh.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Presto_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Presto-156">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> In 1973 David Willcocks conducted a set for HMV in which all the soprano arias were sung in unison by the boys of the <a href="/wiki/Choir_of_King%27s_College,_Cambridge" title="Choir of King&#39;s College, Cambridge">Choir of King's College, Cambridge</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> and in 1974, for DG, Mackerras conducted a set of Mozart's reorchestrated version, sung in German.<sup id="cite_ref-blyth_95-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blyth-95">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Editions">Editions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Editions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The first published score of 1767, together with Handel's documented adaptations and recompositions of various movements, has been the basis for many performing versions since the composer's lifetime. Modern performances which seek authenticity tend to be based on one of three 20th-century performing editions.<sup id="cite_ref-Bix_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bix-116">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> These all use different methods of numbering movements:
</p>
<ul><li>The Novello Edition, edited by Watkins Shaw, first published as a vocal score in 1959, revised and issued 1965. This uses the numbering first used in the Prout edition of 1902.<sup id="cite_ref-Bix_116-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bix-116">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The Bärenreiter Edition, edited by John Tobin, published in 1965, which forms the basis of the <i>Messiah</i> numbering in <a href="/wiki/Bernd_Baselt" title="Bernd Baselt">Bernd Baselt</a>'s catalogue (HWV) of Handel's works, published in 1984.<sup id="cite_ref-Bix_116-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bix-116">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The Peters Edition, edited by Donald Burrows, vocal score published 1972, which uses an adaptation of the numbering devised by Kurt Soldan.<sup id="cite_ref-Bix_116-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bix-116">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The Van Camp Edition, edited by Leonard Van Camp, published by Roger Dean Publishing, 1993 rev. 1995 (now Lorenz pub.).</li>
<li>The Oxford University Press edition by Clifford Bartlett, 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The Carus-Verlag Edition, edited by Ton Koopman and Jan H. Siemons, published in 2009 (using the HWV numbering).</li></ul>
<p>The edition edited by <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Chrysander" title="Friedrich Chrysander">Friedrich Chrysander</a> and <a href="/wiki/Max_Seiffert" title="Max Seiffert">Max Seiffert</a> for the <a href="/wiki/H%C3%A4ndel-Gesellschaft" title="Händel-Gesellschaft">Deutsche Händel-Gesellschaft</a> (Berlin, 1902) is not a general performing edition, but has been used as a basis of scholarship and research.<sup id="cite_ref-Bix_116-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bix-116">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to Mozart's well-known reorchestration, arrangements for larger orchestral forces exist by <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Aynsley_Goossens" title="Eugene Aynsley Goossens">Goossens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Davis_(conductor)" title="Andrew Davis (conductor)">Andrew Davis</a>; both have been recorded at least once, on the <a href="/wiki/RCA_Records" title="RCA Records">RCA</a><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Chandos_Records" title="Chandos Records">Chandos</a><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> labels respectively.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Letters_and_writings_of_George_Frideric_Handel" title="Letters and writings of George Frideric Handel">Letters and writings of George Frideric Handel</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Scratch_Messiah" title="Scratch Messiah">Scratch Messiah</a></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Messiah_(Handel)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Since its earliest performances the work has often been referred to, incorrectly, as "The <i>Messiah</i>". The article is absent from the proper title.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The description "Sinfony" is taken from Handel's autograph score.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It is possible that Delaney was alluding to the fact that Cibber was, at that time, involved in a scandalous divorce suit.<sup id="cite_ref-Smithsonian_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smithsonian-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Anthony_Hicks" title="Anthony Hicks">Anthony Hicks</a> gives a slightly different instrumentation: 14 violins and 6 violas.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-76">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Swieten provided Mozart with a London publication of Handel's original orchestration (published by Randal &amp; Abell), as well as a German translation of the English libretto, compiled and created by <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Gottlieb_Klopstock" title="Friedrich Gottlieb Klopstock">Friedrich Gottlieb Klopstock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christoph_Daniel_Ebeling" title="Christoph Daniel Ebeling">Christoph Daniel Ebeling</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-80"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-80">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A repeat performance was given in the <a href="/wiki/Esterh%C3%A1za" class="mw-redirect" title="Esterháza">Esterháza</a> court on 7 April 1789,<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> and between the year of Mozart's death (1791) and 1800, there were four known performances of Mozart's re-orchestrated <i>Messiah</i> in Vienna: 5 April 1795, 23 March 1799, 23 December 1799 and 24 December 1799.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-83"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-83">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Hiller was long thought to have revised Mozart's scoring substantially before the score was printed. Ebenezer Prout pointed out that the edition was published as "F. G. &#32;&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>&#93; Händels Oratorium Der Messias, nach W. A. Mozarts Bearbeitung" – "nach" meaning <i>after</i> rather than <i>in</i> Mozart's arrangement. Prout noted that a Mozart edition of another Handel work, <i><a href="/wiki/Alexander%27s_Feast_(Handel)" title="Alexander&#39;s Feast (Handel)">Alexander's Feast</a></i> published in accordance with Mozart's manuscript, was printed as "mit neuer Bearbeitung von W. A. Mozart" ("with new arrangement by W. A. Mozart)."<sup id="cite_ref-prout1_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prout1-81">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> When Mozart's original manuscript subsequently came to light it was found that Hiller's changes were not extensive.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-97"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-97">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Many of the editions before 1902, including Mozart's, derived from the earliest printed edition of the score, known as the Walsh Edition, published in 1767.<sup id="cite_ref-prout1_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prout1-81">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-110"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-110">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 1966 an edition by John Tobin was published.<sup id="cite_ref-dean_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dean-109">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> More recent editions have included those edited by Donald Burrows (Edition Peters, 1987) and Clifford Bartlett (Oxford University Press, 1999).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-142"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-142">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The numbers customarily omitted were: from Part II, "Unto which of the angels"; "Let all the angels of God worship Him"; and "Thou art gone up on high"; and from Part III, "Then shall be brought to pass"; "O death, where is thy sting?", "But thanks be to God"; and "If God be for us".<sup id="cite_ref-rg_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rg-141">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-143"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-143">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This recording was monophonic and issued on commercial CD by PRT in 1986; Scherchen re-recorded Messiah in stereo in 1959 using Vienna forces; this was issued on LP by Westminster and on commercial CD by Deutsche Grammophon in 2001. Both recordings have appeared on other labels in both LP and CD formats. A copyright-free transfer of the 1954 version (digitized from original vinyl discs by Nixa Records) is available on YouTube: <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=zhb3s4E8-CA">part 1</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=gfQnICqY3j4">part 2</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=M48k_QBXKeU">part 3</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-146"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-146">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Davis set uses a chorus of 40 singers and an orchestra of 39 players;<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> the Mackerras set uses similarly sized forces, but with fewer strings and more wind players.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-153"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-153">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A 1997 recording under Harry Christophers employed a chorus of 19 and an orchestra of 20.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> In 1993, the Scholars Baroque Ensemble released a version with 14 singers including soloists.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
